وقيد بالاشتباه لأنه لو صلى في الصحراء إلى جهة من غير شك ولا تحر، إن تبين أنه أصاب أو كان أكبر رأيه أو لم يظهر من حاله شيء حتى ذهب عن الموضع فصلاته جائزة، وإن تبين أنه أخطأ أو كان أكبر رأيه فعليه الإعادة . وقيد بالتحري لأن من صلى ممن اشتبهت عليه بلا تحر فعليه الإعادة إلا أن علم بعد الفراغ أنه أصاب لأن ما افترض لغيره يشترط حصوله لا تحصيله. وإن علم في الصلاة أنه أصاب يستقبل خلافاً لأبي يوسف لما ذكرنا . قلنا : حالته قويت بالعلم وبناء القوي على الضعيف لا يجوز أما لو تحرى صلى إلى غير جهة التحري ففي الخلاصة والخانية عن أبي حنيفة أنه يخشى عليه الكفر لإعراضه عن القبلة. وفي الذخيرة: اختلف المشايخ في كفره لأنه صارت قبلة في حقه. وفي الظهيرية : وظن بعض أصحابنا أن الجهة التي أدى إليها التحري قبلة على الحقيقة، وعندنا هذا غير مرضي ففيه قول بأن كل مجتهد يصيب الحق لا محالة ولا نقول به لكن المجتهد يخطىء مرة ويصيب أخرى اهـ . مشكلة وأما صلاته فلا تجزئه وإن أصاب مطلقاً خلافاً لأبي يوسف. وفي فتح القدير : على قولهما لأن تعليلهما في هذه وهو أن القبلة في حقه جهة التحري وقد تركها يقتضي الفساد مطلقاً في صورة ترك التحري لأن ترك جهة التحري تصدق مع ترك التحري هذه. وتعليلهما في تلك بأن ما فرض لغيره يشترط مجرد حصوله كالسعي يقتضي الصحة في وعلى هذا لو صلى في ثوب وعنده أنه نجس ثم ظهر أنه طاهر أو صلى وعنده أنه محدث فظهر أنه متوضيء، أو صلى الفرض وعنده أن الوقت لم يدخل فظهر أنه كان قد دخل، لا يجزئه لأنه لما حكم بفساد صلاته بناء على دليل شرعي وهو تحريه فلا ينقلب جائزاً إذا ظهر خلافه. وهذا التعليل يجري في مسألة العدول عن جهة التحري إذا ظهر صوابه، وبه يندفع الإشكال الذي أوردناه لأن دليل الشرع على الفساد هو التحري أو اعتقاد الفساد عن التحري فإذا حكم الفساد دليل شرعي لزم وذلك منتف في صورة ترك التحري فكان ثبوت الفساد فيها قبل ظهور الصواب إنما هو لمجرد اعتقاده الفساد فيؤاخذ باعتقاده الذي ليس بدليل إذا لم
غيره في الليلة المظلمة من غير إيذاء كما شاهدنا في أكثر المواضع، فلا يجوز التحري في مسجد. كذا في المفتاح. قوله لما ذكرنا) أي أن ما افترض لغيره الخ. وهو تعليل لقول أبي يوسف رحمه الله . قوله : (وأما صلاته) أي صلاة المصلي إلى غير جهة تحرية قوله : ( وإن أصاب مطلقاً) لينظر ما المراد بهذا الإطلاق، ولعل المراد به سواء تبين أنه أصاب في الصلاة أو بعدها تأمل. قوله : (يقتضي الفساد مطلقاً) أي سواء علم بعد الفراغ أنه أصاب أو لم يعلم. قوله : (إنما هو لمجرد اعتقاده الفساد الخ) فيه بحث لأن غاية ما ثبت في صورة ترك التحري عدم الجزم وذلك لا يستلزم اعتقاد الفساد، ومجرد
Chapter of Prayer - Section on Conditions of Prayer. And the moon and other than that; as for the subtleties of astronomy and the forms of fixed stars, he is excused for ignorance of them. So the summary is that the place for taharrī is when one is unable to face the qibla due to the obliteration of landmarks, accumulation of darkness, and density of clouds, as the author mentioned in his Kafi, and it supports what is in al-Zahiriyya that if the sky is clear, taharrī is not permissible and ignorance is not excused. And the commentator mentioned that taharrī is not permissible with mihrabs. And in al-Zahiriyya: A man is confused about the qibla in the mosque and there is no one who knows the qibla. It is said in al-Usul: it is permissible for him to do taharrī because he is unable to ask someone, so he becomes like one in a desert. And the imams of Balkh, among them al-Faqih Abu Ja'far, said: Prayer with taharrī is not permissible for him, and he gave the reason: This is a religious incident, so it is considered by analogy to a worldly incident: if a worldly incident occurred to him, he would seek help from the neighbors of the mosque; likewise here he must seek help from them. And if he is in his own mosque, some said it is like a house, taharrī is not permissible. And some said: his mosque and another's mosque are the same. And Abu Ja'far narrated from Salam ibn Hakim that he said: The mihrabs of Khurasan are all set towards the Black Stone, and the Black Stone is towards the left side of the Ka'ba. Whoever faces the Ka'ba and turns his face to the left side of the Ka'ba, his face falls towards Mount Abu Qubays; and whoever turns his face to its right, his face falls towards the Ka'ba. For this reason it was said he must incline to its right. He said: And the mihrabs of the whole world were all set by taharrī, even Mina, and nothing more was added. This contradicts what was transmitted from Abu Bakr al-Razi regarding the mihrab of Medina, that it is decisive because the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) installed it by revelation, unlike other places. It was even said that the mihrab of Mina was set by taharrī and signs, and it is the closest place to Mecca. So it becomes clear that their saying 'for a non-Makkan, hitting its direction' is not absolute, but rather for a non-Madani, for the Madani is like the Makkan: he is required to hit its exact direction, as stated in al-Siraj al-Wahhaj also. And he spoke in absolute terms about confusion, so it includes when one is in Mecca or Medina, such as being imprisoned and having no one to ask, so he prays by taharrī, then it becomes clear that he was mistaken. It is narrated from Muhammad that he does not have to repeat, and al-Razi used to say: he must repeat because he is certain of the mistake if in Mecca or Medina, and the first is better. Similarly in al-Zahiriyya and in Fatawa Qadi Khan: A man prayed in a mosque on a dark night by taharrī, and it became clear that he prayed towards other than the qibla; his prayer is valid because he does not have to knock on the doors of people to ask about the qibla, and he cannot know the qibla by touching the wall and the walls because if the wall is decorated, he cannot distinguish the mihrab from other things, and perhaps there is a harmful vermin there, so taharrī is permissible for him. And it is restricted by confusion because if he prayed in the desert facing a direction without doubt or taharrī, if it becomes clear that he hit it, or his stronger opinion was correct, or nothing appeared from his condition until he left the place, his prayer is valid; and if it becomes clear that he was mistaken, or his stronger opinion was mistaken, he must repeat. And it is restricted by taharrī because if one who is confused prays without taharrī, he must repeat, except if he knows after finishing that he hit it, because what is prescribed for others requires its attainment, not its acquisition. And if he knows during the prayer that he hit it, he should reorient, contrary to Abu Yusuf, because of what we mentioned. We said: his condition is strengthened by knowledge, and building the strong on the weak is not permissible. But if he did taharrī and prayed to other than the direction of his taharrī, then in al-Khulasa and al-Khaniyya from Abu Hanifa: he fears kufr for him due to his turning away from the qibla. And in al-Dhakhira: The scholars differed about his unbelief because it became the qibla in his regard. And in al-Zahiriyya: Some of our companions thought that the direction to which taharrī leads is the true qibla, and in our view this is not satisfactory. There is a saying that every mujtahid hits the truth inevitably, and we do not say this, but the mujtahid errs once and hits another time. This is problematic. As for his prayer, it is not valid even if he hits it absolutely, contrary to Abu Yusuf. And in Fath al-Qadir: According to his two statements, because their reasoning in this is that the qibla in his regard is the direction of taharrī, and he has abandoned it, necessitates invalidity absolutely in the case of abandoning taharrī, because abandoning the direction of taharrī is realized with abandoning taharrī in this. And their reasoning in that is that what is prescribed for others requires mere attainment, like sa'y, necessitates validity in... And upon this, if he prayed in a garment thinking it was impure, then it appeared pure, or prayed thinking he had impurity, then appeared to have wudu, or prayed the obligatory prayer thinking the time had not entered, then appeared that it had entered, it is not valid because when he judged its invalidity based on a legal proof, which is his taharrī, it does not become valid when the opposite appears. This reasoning applies to the issue of deviating from the direction of taharrī if his correctness appears, and with it the problem we presented is repelled, because the legal proof for invalidity is the taharrī or the belief of invalidity from the taharrī. So when invalidity is judged by a legal proof, it is necessary, and that is absent in the case of abandoning taharrī. So the establishment of invalidity in it before the appearance of correctness is only due to his mere belief of invalidity, so he is taken to task for his belief which is not a proof when there is no other in the dark night without harm, as we have observed in most places. So taharrī is not permissible in a mosque. Likewise in al-Miftah. His saying: 'because of what we mentioned' i.e., that what is prescribed for others requires its attainment, etc. This is the reasoning for the statement of Abu Yusuf. His saying: 'As for his prayer' i.e., the prayer of the one who prays to other than the direction of his taharrī. His saying: 'even if he hits it absolutely' we look to see what is meant by this absolute; perhaps what is meant is whether it becomes clear that he hit it during the prayer or after it, ponder. His saying: 'necessitates invalidity absolutely' i.e., whether he knows after finishing that he hit it or does not know. His saying: 'it is only due to his mere belief of invalidity etc.' In it there is discussion, because the utmost established in the case of abandoning taharrī is the lack of certainty, and that does not necessitate belief of invalidity, and mere...
Source pages p. 500

Commentary
This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq discusses the rulings on taharrī (estimation) for determining the qibla when one is uncertain. It opens by stating that taharrī is the method used when one cannot face the qibla due to loss of landmarks, darkness, or clouds. The author notes a preference for the view in al-Zahiriyyah that if the sky is clear, taharrī is not permissible and ignorance is not excused. The commentator adds that taharrī is not permissible when mihrabs are present. The text then presents an intra-Hanafi dispute: in a mosque where no one can be asked, some authorities (like the author of al-Usul) allow taharrī, while the imams of Balkh, including Abu Ja'far, forbid it, analogizing to worldly incidents where one would seek help from neighbors. They also discuss whether one's own mosque differs from another's. A narration from Salam ibn Hakim states that the mihrabs of Khurasan were set facing the Black Stone, which itself is to the left of the Ka'ba, so one must incline to the right. The mihrab of Medina, however, is decisive because it was set by the Prophet (peace be upon him) by revelation, so no taharrī is allowed there. The author then clarifies that for a non-Makkan, hitting the general direction is sufficient except for a Madani, who must hit the exact direction. In cases of confusion (ishtibah), even in Mecca or Medina, if one is imprisoned and prays by taharrī then realizes error, Muhammad reports no repeat is needed, while al-Razi insists on repeat; the first is preferred. Furthermore, a man praying in a mosque on a dark night by taharrī is valid even if he faces the wrong direction, because he is excused. The passage then restricts the ruling to cases of doubt: if one prays in the desert without doubt and without taharrī, and it turns out correct, it is valid; if mistaken, he must repeat. Also, if one prays without taharrī while confused, he must repeat unless he later knows he was correct. If during prayer he realizes his direction was correct, he should reorient (istiqbal), contrary to Abu Yusuf. A severe warning is given: if one does taharrī but then prays to a direction other than his taharrī, Abu Hanifa fears kufr for him. The text ends with a discussion of the reasoning: the prayer is invalid even if he hits the true qibla, because his taharrī acted as a legal indicator; this is contrary to Abu Yusuf. The reasoning extends to analogous cases like praying in a garment thought impure, etc.

Key Terms
التحري (al-taharrī): Estimation or exertion of effort to determine the correct direction of the qibla when one is uncertain.
الاشتباه (al-ishtibāh): Confusion or uncertainty about the direction of the qibla.
المحراب (al-mihrāb): The niche in a mosque indicating the direction of prayer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following situations permits taharrī according to the Hanafi school?
A) A clear, starry night in the desert.
B) A mosque with a clearly visible mihrab.
C) A dark, cloudy night in an unfamiliar area.
D) Being with someone who knows the qibla.

Key Takeaway
Taharrī is a concession for specific situations of uncertainty; it is not a general alternative to determining qibla.

Salah — Prayer
intermediate

Taharrī in a Mosque: An Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on taharrī in a mosque.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the two positions on taharrī in a mosque when no one can be asked, and identify the preferred view.

Core Idea
When lost in a mosque, some Hanafis allow taharrī; others require asking neighbours.

What if you are inside a mosque and uncertain of the qibla, with no one to ask? The Hanafi school has an internal dispute. This lesson presents the arguments of the scholars of Balkh against the general Hanafi position that permits taharrī when one cannot ask.

Guided Path
1
State the general Hanafi position
In al-Usul, it is said that if one is confused in the mosque and cannot find anyone to ask, taharrī is permissible because he is like one in a desert (mafazah).
This is the position of the majority of Hanafis.
2
Present the opposing view
The imams of Balkh, particularly Abu Ja'far, forbid taharrī in this scenario. They analogize the situation to a worldly incident: one would seek help from neighbors, so here one must seek help from people in the mosque.
3
Consider the sub-dispute about one's own mosque
Some say if it is his own mosque, it is like a house and taharrī is not allowed. Others say his mosque and any mosque are the same.
4
Examine the practical ruling
The passage later cites Fatawa Qadi Khan: a man who prayed by taharrī on a dark night in a mosque and found he was wrong—his prayer is valid because he cannot knock on doors or touch walls, and there may be harm.
This supports the permissibility view.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The commentary seems to favor permissibility, as the example of the dark night mosque is given and the ruling is that the prayer is valid.

Source Text

والقمر وغير ذلك، أما دقائق علم الهيئة وصور النجوم الثوابت فهو معذور في الجهل بها اهـ. فالحاصل أن محل التحري أن يعجز عن الاستقبال بانطماس الأعلام وتراكم الظلام وتضام الغمام كما ذكره المصنف في كافيه، وهو يرجح ما في الظهيرية من أن السماء إذا كانت مصحية لا يجوز التحري ولا يعذر بالجهل. وذكر الشارح أنه لا يجوز التحري مع المحاريب. وفي الظهيرية : رجل اشتبهت عليه القبلة في المسجد ولم يكن أحد يعرفه القبلة قال في الأصول : يجوز له التحري لأنه عجز عمن يسأله فصار كالمفازة. وقال أئمة بلخ منهم الفقيه أبو جعفر : لا تجوز له الصلاة بالتحري وعلل فقال : إن هذه نائبة العقبي فتعتبر بنائبة الدنيا ولو حدثت به نائبة الدنيا فإنه يستغيث بجيران المسجد كذلك ههنا يجب أن يستغيث بهم. وإن كان في مسجد نفسه قال بعضهم هو كالبيت لا يجوز له التحري. وقال بعضهم : مسجده ومسجد غيره سواء. وروى أبو جعفر عن سلام بن حكيم أنه قال : محاريب خراسان كلها منصوبة إلى الحجر الأسود والحجر الأسود إلى ميسرة الكعبة ومن توجه إلى الكعبة ومال بوجهه إلى ميسرة الكعبة وقع وجهه إلى جبل أبي قبيس، ومن مال بوجهه إلى يمينها وقع وجهه إلى الكعبة، ولهذا قيل يجب أن يميل إلى يمينها . قال : ومحاريب الدنيا كلها نصبت بالتحري حتى منى ولم يزد عليه شيئاً، وهذا خلاف ما نقل عن أبي بكر الرازي في محراب المدينة أنه مقطوع به فإنه إنما نصبه رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم بالوحي بخلاف سائر البقاع حتى قيل أن محراب منى نصب بالتحري والعلامات وهو أقرب المواضع إلى مكة .اهـ. وبهذا تبين أن قولهم «لغير المكي أصابة جهتها ليس على إطلاقه بل في غير المدني فإن المدني كالمكي يفترض عليه إصابة عينها صرح به في السراج الوهاج أيضاً. وأطلق في الاشتباه فشمل ما إذا كان بمكة أو بالمدينة بأن كان محبوساً ولم يكن بحضرته من يسأله فصلى بالتحري ثم تبين أنه أخطأ، روي عن محمد أنه لا إعادة عليه، وكان الرازي يقول : تلزمه الإعادة لأنه تيقن بالخطأ إذا كان بمكة أو بالمدينة والأول أحسن كذا في الظهيرية وفي فتاوى قاضيخان رجل صلى في المسجد في ليلة مظلمة بالتحري فتبين أنه صلى إلى غير القبلة جازت صلاته لأنه ليس له أن يقرع أبواب

And it is restricted by confusion because if he prayed in the desert facing a direction without doubt or taharrī, if it becomes clear that he hit it, or his stronger opinion was correct, or nothing appeared from his condition until he left the place, his prayer is valid; and if it becomes clear that he was mistaken, or his stronger opinion was mistaken, he must repeat. Commentary
This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq discusses the rulings on taharrī (estimation) for determining the qibla when one is uncertain. It opens by stating that taharrī is the method used when one cannot face the qibla due to loss of landmarks, darkness, or clouds. The author notes a preference for the view in al-Zahiriyyah that if the sky is clear, taharrī is not permissible and ignorance is not excused. The commentator adds that taharrī is not permissible when mihrabs are present. The text then presents an intra-Hanafi dispute: in a mosque where no one can be asked, some authorities (like the author of al-Usul) allow taharrī, while the imams of Balkh, including Abu Ja'far, forbid it, analogizing to worldly incidents where one would seek help from neighbors. They also discuss whether one's own mosque differs from another's. A narration from Salam ibn Hakim states that the mihrabs of Khurasan were set facing the Black Stone, which itself is to the left of the Ka'ba, so one must incline to the right. The mihrab of Medina, however, is decisive because it was set by the Prophet (peace be upon him) by revelation, so no taharrī is allowed there. The author then clarifies that for a non-Makkan, hitting the general direction is sufficient except for a Madani, who must hit the exact direction. In cases of confusion (ishtibah), even in Mecca or Medina, if one is imprisoned and prays by taharrī then realizes error, Muhammad reports no repeat is needed, while al-Razi insists on repeat; the first is preferred. Furthermore, a man praying in a mosque on a dark night by taharrī is valid even if he faces the wrong direction, because he is excused. The passage then restricts the ruling to cases of doubt: if one prays in the desert without doubt and without taharrī, and it turns out correct, it is valid; if mistaken, he must repeat. Also, if one prays without taharrī while confused, he must repeat unless he later knows he was correct. If during prayer he realizes his direction was correct, he should reorient (istiqbal), contrary to Abu Yusuf. A severe warning is given: if one does taharrī but then prays to a direction other than his taharrī, Abu Hanifa fears kufr for him. The text ends with a discussion of the reasoning: the prayer is invalid even if he hits the true qibla, because his taharrī acted as a legal indicator; this is contrary to Abu Yusuf. The reasoning extends to analogous cases like praying in a garment thought impure, etc.

Key Terms
المفازة (al-mafāzah): A desert or wilderness where no one is around to ask.
النائبة (al-nā'ibah): An incident or calamity that befalls a person.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the preferred view in the Hanafi school, what should a person do if they are in a mosque, uncertain of the qibla, and no one is around to ask?
A) He must leave the mosque and find someone.
B) He may perform taharrī and pray.
C) He must knock on all doors until he finds someone.
D) He must wait until someone arrives.

Key Takeaway
Despite an intra-Hanafi dispute, the relied-upon ruling is that taharrī is permissible in a mosque when one cannot ask anyone.

Salah — Prayer
intermediate

Praying Without Ijtihad When Qibla is Unclear: Abu Hanifa vs Abu Yusuf
Commentary explaining the condition of uncertainty and rulings for those who pray without doubt or ijtihad

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the positions of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf on praying without ijtihad when the qibla is unclear, and apply the Hanafi ruling on repetition and turning during the prayer.

Core Idea
Without ijtihad when qibla is unclear, the prayer is invalid unless you later know you were correct.

This lesson delves into a key disagreement between Abu Hanifa and his student Abu Yusuf on the validity of prayer when one neglects ijtihad (attempting to find the qibla) under uncertainty. The passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains the conditions, exceptions, and the underlying reasoning, including an analogy to other cases of doubt. Understanding this dispute sharpens your grasp of how Hanafi jurists balance obligation, knowledge, and legal presumptions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the General Hanafi Ruling
State the baseline: when the qibla is unclear (ishtibah), ijtihad is wajib. If one prays without any ijtihad or doubt, the prayer is invalid unless he later discovers he was correct.
The passage distinguishes between praying in the desert without doubt (valid if correct, repeat if wrong) and praying with uncertainty but no ijtihad (repeat unless correctness known after).
2
Locate the Point of Disagreement
Abu Yusuf differs on the scenario where, during the prayer, the person realizes he was facing the correct direction. Abu Hanifa says he must turn and repeat if he doesn't; Abu Yusuf says he may continue and his prayer is valid.
This is the central khilaf: does knowledge during prayer change the ruling?
3
Analyze Abu Hanifa's Proof
Abu Hanifa argues that knowledge strengthens the state; you cannot build the strong (known correct direction) upon the weak (previous direction without ijtihad). The prayer must be reoriented.
The reasoning rests on qiyas: if you build a strong structure on a weak foundation, it's invalid.
4
Examine the Analogical Extensions
The passage extends the same reasoning to cases like praying in a garment thought to be impure (then found pure), or thinking one is without wudu (then found with wudu), or praying before thinking the time had entered (then found entered). In all, the prayer is invalid because the initial judgment was based on a legal evidence (ijtihad or belief).
The author uses these analogies to reinforce that the ijtihad (or its absence) creates a legal presumption that cannot be overturned by later knowledge.
5
Determine the Mufta Bihi
The relied-upon position in the Hanafi school is Abu Hanifa's: one must turn upon learning correctness during prayer, and if he doesn't, he must repeat. Abu Yusuf's view is recorded but not followed.
This is confirmed by the commentator's reasoning and later authorities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و قيد بالاشتباه لأنه لو صلى في الصحراء إلى جهة من غير شك ولا تحر، إن تبين أنه هي أصاب أو كان أكبر رأيه أو لم يظهر من حاله شيء حتى ذهب عن الموضع فصلاته جائزة، وإن تبين أنه أخطأ أو كان أكبر رأيه فعليه الإعادة . وقيد بالتحري لأن من صلى ممن اشتبهت عليه بلا تحر فعليه الإعادة إلا أن علم بعد الفراغ أنه أصاب لأن ما افترض لغيره يشترط حصوله لا تحصيله. وإن علم في الصلاة أنه أصاب يستقبل خلافاً لأبي يوسف لما ذكرنا . قلنا : حالته قويت بالعلم وبناء القوي على الضعيف لا يجوز أما لو تحرى صلى إلى غير جهة التحري ففي الخلاصة والخانية عن أبي حنيفة أنه يخشى عليه الكفر لإعراضه عن القبلة. وفي الذخيرة: اختلف المشايخ في كفره لأنه صارت قبلة في حقه. وفي الظهيرية : وظن بعض أصحابنا أن الجهة التي أدى إليها التحري قبلة على الحقيقة، وعندنا هذا غير مرضي ففيه قول بأن كل مجتهد يصيب الحق لا محالة ولا نقول به لكن المجتهد يخطىء مرة ويصيب أخرى اهـ . مشكلة وأما صلاته فلا تجزئه وإن أصاب مطلقاً خلافاً لأبي يوسف. وفي فتح القدير : على قولهما لأن تعليلهما في هذه وهو أن القبلة في حقه جهة التحري وقد تركها يقتضي الفساد مطلقاً في صورة ترك التحري لأن ترك جهة التحري تصدق مع ترك التحري هذه. وتعليلهما في تلك بأن ما فرض لغيره يشترط مجرد حصوله كالسعي يقتضي الصحة في وعلى هذا لو صلى في ثوب وعنده أنه نجس ثم ظهر أنه طاهر أو صلى وعنده أنه محدث فظهر أنه متوضيء، أو صلى الفرض وعنده أن الوقت لم يدخل فظهر أنه كان قد دخل، لا يجزئه لأنه لما حكم بفساد صلاته بناء على دليل شرعي وهو تحريه فلا ينقلب جائزاً إذا ظهر خلافه. وهذا التعليل يجري في مسألة العدول عن جهة التحري إذا ظهر صوابه، وبه يندفع الإشكال الذي أوردناه لأن دليل الشرع على الفساد هو التحري أو اعتقاد الفساد عن التحري فإذا حكم الفساد دليل شرعي لزم وذلك منتف في صورة ترك التحري فكان ثبوت الفساد فيها قبل ظهور الصواب إنما هو لمجرد اعتقاده الفساد فيؤاخذ باعتقاده الذي ليس بدليل إذا لم يكن عن تحر.
And it is restricted by uncertainty because if he prayed in the desert towards a direction without doubt and without ijtihad, if it becomes clear that it was the [correct] direction, or his prevailing opinion [was correct], or nothing became apparent from his situation until he left the place, then his prayer is valid. If it becomes clear that he was wrong or his prevailing opinion [was wrong], then repetition is upon him. And it is restricted by ijtihad because one who prays while the qibla is unclear without ijtihad must repeat unless he knows after completion that he was correct, because what is prescribed for something else requires only its attainment, not its attempted attainment. If he knows during the prayer that he was correct, he should turn [to the qibla], contrary to Abu Yusuf, due to what we mentioned. We said: his state becomes strong through knowledge, and building the strong upon the weak is not permissible. As for if he performed ijtihad and prayed to other than the direction of his ijtihad, then in al-Khulasa and al-Khaniyya from Abu Hanifa that it is feared for him to be disbelief due to his turning away from the qibla. In al-Dhakhira: the scholars differed concerning his disbelief because it became the qibla for him. In al-Zahiriyya: some of our companions thought that the direction to which his ijtihad led is the real qibla, and this is not satisfactory with us, so there is a saying that every mujtahid is correct inevitably, but we do not say that; rather the mujtahid errs once and hits another time. Problematic. As for his prayer, it does not suffice him even if he was correct absolutely, contrary to Abu Yusuf. And in Fath al-Qadir: according to them because their reasoning in this [case]—that the qibla for him is the direction of his ijtihad and he abandoned it—necessitates corruption absolutely in the case of abandoning ijtihad, because abandoning the direction of ijtihad is true with abandoning ijtihad. And their reasoning in that [case]—that what is prescribed for something else requires only its attainment, like sa'y—necessitates validity. So based on this, if he prayed in a garment while he thought it was impure, then it became clear that it was pure; or he prayed while he thought he was in a state of minor impurity, then it became clear that he was in ablution; or he prayed the obligatory prayer while he thought the time had not entered, then it became clear that it had entered: it does not suffice him, because when he ruled the invalidity of his prayer based on a legal evidence—which is his ijtihad—it does not become valid when its contrary appears. This reasoning applies to the issue of turning away from the direction of ijtihad when his correctness appears, and by it the problem we raised is repelled, because the legal evidence for invalidity is the ijtihad or the belief of invalidity from ijtihad. So when invalidity is ruled by a legal evidence, it becomes binding, and that is absent in the case of abandoning ijtihad. So the establishment of invalidity in it before the appearance of correctness is only due to his mere belief of invalidity, so he is held accountable for his belief, which is not evidence, if it was not from ijtihad.
Source pages p. 501

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the ruling on a person who prays without ijtihad when the qibla is unclear. The commentary first distinguishes two scenarios: one who prays without any doubt or ijtihad (e.g., in the desert), and one who prays with uncertainty but without ijtihad. For the first, if he later discovers he was correct, his prayer is valid; if wrong, he must repeat. For the second, he must repeat unless he discovers correctness after completion, because the obligation is to actually face the qibla, not just to attempt it. Abu Yusuf disagreed regarding the ruling if one realizes during prayer that he was correct: Abu Hanifa says he should turn, while Abu Yusuf says he may continue. The commentator explains Abu Hanifa's reasoning: knowledge strengthens the state, and building the strong upon the weak is not allowed. The passage then discusses the case where one performs ijtihad but prays to a different direction than his ijtihad: Abu Hanifa fears disbelief due to turning away from the qibla that became obligatory for him. The scholars differed on whether such a person becomes a disbeliever. Finally, the passage provides an analogy: if one prays thinking his garment is impure, or that he is in a state of impurity, or that the time has not entered, his prayer is not valid even if the contrary appears. The author concludes that the legal evidence for invalidity is the ijtihad or the belief derived from it; without ijtihad, the belief itself is not sufficient evidence to invalidate.

Key Terms
اشتباه (ishtibāh): Uncertainty about the direction of the qibla; the condition for requiring ijtihad.
تحري (taḥarrī): The process of attempting to determine the correct qibla direction through reasoning and signs.
التحري (al-taḥarrī): The specific ijtihad to determine qibla; the direction concluded becomes the qibla for that person.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling for a person who prays in the desert without any doubt or ijtihad, and later finds out he was facing the wrong direction?
A) His prayer is valid and he does not need to repeat.
B) He must repeat the prayer.
C) His prayer is valid only if he had a prevailing opinion.
D) He must make up the prayer with qada' but no sin.

Key Takeaway
Prayer without ijtihad when the qibla is unclear is invalid unless you learn after completion that you were correct, and if you learn during the prayer you must turn (Abu Hanifa).

Hanafi Rulings for Undetermined Qibla
Various scholarly opinions on what to do when one cannot determine qibla through ijtihad

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on praying when qibla determination by ijtihad is inconclusive and identify the key cases where the prayer remains valid.

Core Idea
When ijtihad fails to indicate qibla, multiple valid options exist: delay, pray four times, or choose a direction.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq compiles various Hanafi rulings on the situation where a person cannot determine the qibla through ijtihad. It presents opinions from Fatawa al-Atabi, al-Zahiriyya, and al-Khulasa, as well as a later disagreement about shifting direction during prayer. Understanding these options is essential for a mufti or traveler facing uncertainty.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core scenario
The passage addresses a person who performs ijtihad to find qibla but ends up with no preference among directions. Understand that this is the starting point for all rulings.
2
List the three opinions from Fatawa al-Atabi
From Fatawa al-Atabi, the passage records three opinions: delay the prayer, pray to all four directions, or give the person a choice. Note these as primary options.
These are reported as 'qīla' (it is said), indicating they are not necessarily the strongest views.
3
Understand the default validity from al-Zahiriyya
Al-Zahiriyya states that if the person prays to any direction after ijtihad, the prayer is valid regardless of whether he later discovers he was correct, wrong, or remains uncertain. This is a key principle.
This implies that even without a preferred direction, a single prayer to any direction is valid.
4
Note Imam Muhammad's opinion
Imam Muhammad permits praying four complete fard prayers to four cardinal directions. This is a cautious approach that ensures one of them faces the correct qibla.
This is reported from al-Khulasa.
5
Recognize the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on shifting direction
Later scholars disagreed on whether one must turn to a new direction if his ijtihad changes during prayer. Some say continue, others say face the new direction. Also, if he turns and remembers a missed sajda, the prayer becomes invalid.
This demonstrates that even within the school, there is room for difference.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي فتاوى العتابي : تحرى فلم يقع تحريه على شيء قيل يؤخر، وقيل يصلي إلى أربع جهات، وقي يخير. وفي الظهيرية ولو تحرى رجل واستوى الحالان عنده ولم يتيقن بشيء ولكن صلى إلى إن ظهر أنه أصاب القبلة جاز، وإن ظهر أنه اخطأ فكذلك، وإن لم يظهر له شيء جازت صلاته. وفي الخلاصة : وعن محمد لو صلى أربع ركعات إلى أربع جهات جاز. ثم اختلف المتأخرون فيما إذا تحول رأيه إلى الجهة الأولى بالتحري؛ فمنهم من قال الصلاة، ومنهم من يستقبل اهـ . وفي البغية لو صلى إلى جهة بتحر ثم تحول رأيه في الركعة الثانية إلى جهة أخرى فتحوّل وتذكر أنه ترك سجدة من الركعة الأولى فسدت صلاته. وفي الظهيرية ويجوز التحري لسجدة التلاوة كما يجوز للصلاة.
And in Fatawa al-Atabi: he performed ijtihad and his ijtihad did not fall upon anything. It is said: delay [the prayer], and it is said: pray to four directions, and it is said: he is given a choice. In al-Zahiriyya: if a man performed ijtihad and the two states were equal for him and he was not certain of anything, but he prayed to [a direction], then if it appears that he hit the qibla, it is permissible; if it appears that he missed, then likewise; if nothing appears to him, his prayer is permissible. In al-Khulasa: from Muhammad: if he prayed four rak'ahs to four directions, it is permissible. Then the later scholars differed concerning when his opinion shifts to the first direction by ijtihad: some of them said [he continues] the prayer, and some said he should face it. In al-Bughya: if he prayed to a direction by ijtihad, then his opinion shifted in the second rak'ah to another direction, so he turned, and he remembered that he left a prostration from the first rak'ah, his prayer becomes invalid. And in al-Zahiriyya: ijtihad is permissible for the prostration of recitation just as it is permissible for prayer.
Source pages p. 502

Commentary
This passage collects multiple rulings from various Hanafi authorities on the situation where a person cannot determine the qibla through ijtihad (e.g., because all directions seem equally likely). It presents three opinions from Fatawa al-Atabi: delay the prayer until clarity; pray four separate prayers to four directions; or choose any direction. In al-Zahiriyya, it is reported that if one performs ijtihad and the two directions are equal, yet he prays to some direction, his prayer is valid regardless of whether he later discovers he was correct or wrong, or if nothing becomes clear. Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani is reported to have allowed praying four rak'ahs to four directions (i.e., performing the fard prayer four times). Later scholars disagreed on a scenario where a person's ijtihad shifts during the prayer back to the original direction: some say he continues, others say he must face the new direction. If he actually turns in the second rak'ah and then remembers he left a sajda from the first rak'ah, the prayer is invalid due to the disruption. Finally, ijtihad for the direction of prostration of recitation is analogous to that for prayer.

Key Terms
تخيير (takhayyur): Having the option to choose any direction for prayer when ijtihad yields no preference.
سجدة التلاوة (sajdat al-tilāwah): The prostration of recitation performed upon hearing or reciting verses of prostration in the Quran.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Muhammad (from al-Khulasa), what is one permissible action when a person cannot determine the qibla through ijtihad?
A) He must delay the prayer indefinitely.
B) He may pray one prayer to any direction.
C) He may pray four rak'ahs to four directions.
D) He must choose a direction by lots.

Key Takeaway
When ijtihad cannot determine qibla, Hanafi law permits delaying, praying four times to four directions, or choosing a direction, with validity assured by many authorities.

Turning to the Correct Qibla During Prayer: Ruling and Proof
Ruling with proof from Quba' hadith

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on turning to the correct qibla during prayer and explain the Hanafi proof from the Quba' hadith.

Core Idea
If you realize the qibla is wrong during prayer, turn immediately and continue — the prayer remains valid.

In this passage, the author explains what a praying person must do when they discover they are facing the wrong direction. The ruling is based on the famous incident of the Companions in the mosque of Quba', who were praying towards Jerusalem when news came that the qibla had changed to Makkah. They turned around without breaking their prayer. This incident yields important fiqh and usul lessons.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling
The passage begins with a statement: if a person knows of the error during prayer, he turns. This is the core fiqh ruling.
The phrase 'istadara' (he turns) implies a complete turn to face the correct qibla.
2
Locate the Proof (Dalil)
The proof is the incident of the Companions in the mosque of Quba'. They were informed of the change of qibla and turned around as they were.
This incident is recorded in Sahih al-Bukhari and Muslim.
3
Understand the Reasoning
The change of ijtihad is like the change of the Ansar. The action of turning does not invalidate the prayer because the error was unintentional.
Hanafi usul: unintentional errors in non-essential elements do not invalidate.
4
Note the Extracted Principles
The author extracts three usul points: (1) Sunnah can abrogate the Book, (2) naskh only takes effect when it reaches the mukallaf, (3) khabar wahid obligates action.
These principles are derived from the same hadith.
5
Consider the Discussion
The passage includes a debate on whether the Jerusalem qibla was established by Qur'an or Sunnah, but the ruling for turning remains unaffected.
This debate affects how we attribute the ruling, not the practical action.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله (وإن علم به في صلاته استدار أي إن علم بالخطأ لأن تبدل الاجتهاد بمنزلة تبدل الأنصار كانوا يصلون بمسجد قباء إلى بيت المقدس فأخبروا أن قوماً روي من النسخ وقد بتحول القبلة فاستداروا كهيئتهم. وفيه دليل على جواز نسخ الكتاب السنة إذ لا نص على بيت المقدس في القرآن فعلم أنه كان ثابتاً بالسنة ثم نسخ بالكتاب . وعلى أن حكم النسخ لا يثبت حتى يبلغ المكلف وعلى أن خبر الواحد يوجب العمل. كا ذكر الشارح. وفي كون بيت المقدس ثبت التوجه إليه بالسنة فقط بحث بل في القرآن العاليم ما يدل عليه فإنه قال تعالى سيقول السفهاء من الناس ما ولاهم عن قبلتهم التي كانوا عليها [البقرة: ١٤٢] قال بأن تحول رأيه في كل ركعة إلى جهة غير التي صلى إليها قوله (لأنه لما لم يجز التحري الخ) هذا التعليل غير موافق للمغلل ولعل في العبارة سقطاً فليتأمل . قوله : (بل في القرآن العظيم ما يدل عليه فيه نظر لأنه لا نص على بيت المقدس وإنما السنة بينت أن المراد من قبلتهم بيت المقدس على أن ثبوت التوجه إليه لم يكن حاصلاً بهذه الآية بل كان ثابتاً بالسنة وهذه الآية تدل على نسخه. نعم فيها دلالة بعد البيان على مشروعيته قبلها وليس الكلام في مجرد مشروعيته بل في موجبه وهي لم تدل عليه فليتأمل. كذا قاله الشيخ اسمعيل أقول : وفي الجواب الأول نظر لأن الكتاب إذا بينته السنة يكون الحكم مضافاً إلى الكتاب لا إلى السنة كما نبه عليه في العناية عند الكلام على مسح الرأس. نعم يرد على الشارح الزيلعي أن التوجه إلى بيت المقدس من شرائع من قبلنا وهو ثابت بقوله تعالى
His saying: (And if he knows of it in his prayer, he turns) i.e., if he knows of the error, because the change of ijtihad is like the change of the Ansar. They were praying in the mosque of Quba' towards Jerusalem, and they were informed that some people related that the abrogation had occurred and the qibla had changed, so they turned as they were. In it is evidence for the permissibility of the abrogation of the Book by the Sunnah, since there is no text regarding Jerusalem in the Qur'an, so it is known that it was established by the Sunnah, then abrogated by the Book. And that the ruling of abrogation is not established until it reaches the obligated person, and that the report of one person necessitates action. As the commentator mentioned. And regarding that the direction to Jerusalem was established only by the Sunnah, there is investigation; rather, in the Mighty Qur'an there is indication of it, for He said: 'The fools among the people will say: What has turned them from their qibla which they used to face?' [2:142]. He said: that his opinion changes in every rak'a to a direction other than that to which he prayed. His saying: (Because when taharri is not permitted, etc.) This explanation does not agree with the context, and perhaps there is an omission in the wording, so consider. His saying: (Rather, in the Mighty Qur'an there is indication of it) There is consideration in it, because there is no text regarding Jerusalem; rather, the Sunnah explained that the intended meaning of 'their qibla' is Jerusalem. Moreover, the establishment of the direction to it was not obtained by this verse, but was established by the Sunnah, and this verse indicates its abrogation. Yes, it contains an indication after explanation of its legitimacy before it, and the discussion is not about mere legitimacy but about its obligatoriness, and the verse does not indicate that. So consider. Thus said Sheikh Isma'il. I say: In the first answer there is consideration because the Book, when clarified by the Sunnah, the ruling is attributed to the Book, not to the Sunnah, as pointed out in al-‘Inaya when discussing wiping the head. Yes, there is an objection to the commentator al-Zayla'i that the direction to Jerusalem is from the laws of those before us and is established by the saying of Allah (the Most High).
Source pages p. 503

Commentary
This passage discusses the ruling that if a person realizes during prayer that he is facing the wrong qibla, he should turn to the correct direction immediately without breaking his prayer. The proof is the hadith of the Companions in the mosque of Quba' who were praying towards Jerusalem when they received news that the qibla had been changed to Makkah; they turned around in their prayer. The author also extracts several legal principles from this incident: (1) the Sunnah can abrogate the Qur'an (since the initial qibla was established by Sunnah), (2) abrogation is only effective when it reaches the obligated person, and (3) a single narrator report (khabar wahid) is binding for action. The passage then includes a discussion on whether the direction to Jerusalem was established by the Qur'an or only by Sunnah. The commentator points out that the verse 'Say: To Allah belongs the east and the west' can be interpreted, but the consensus is that it was established by Sunnah and then abrogated by the Qur'anic command to face the Ka'ba. The author also notes an objection by Sheikh Isma'il regarding the attribution of rulings when the Sunnah clarifies the Qur'an—the ruling is then attributed to the Qur'an. The passage ends with a brief critique of al-Zayla'i's commentary.

Key Terms
استدارة (istidāra): Turning around to face the correct direction during prayer without breaking it.
نسخ (naskh): Abrogation of a previous legal ruling by a later one; here, the change of qibla from Jerusalem to Makkah.
خبر واحد (khabar wāḥid): A single-narrator hadith that is not mutawatir; the passage argues it is binding for action (wujub al-‘amal).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What should a person do if he realizes during his prayer that he is facing the wrong qibla, according to the passage?
A) He must stop and restart the prayer
B) He should turn to the correct direction and continue
C) He can choose to either continue or restart
D) He must perform sajda al-sahw

Key Takeaway
If you learn of the correct qibla during prayer, turn immediately and continue; the prayer remains valid.

Usul Principles from the Qibla Change: Naskh, Notification, and Khabar Wahid
Derivation of usul principles from Quba' hadith

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how the Quba' hadith demonstrates (1) the permissibility of Sunnah abrogating the Qur'an, (2) the condition of notification for naskh, and (3) the binding force of khabar wahid.

Core Idea
The change of qibla proves three usul principles: Sunnah can abrogate Qur'an, naskh requires notification, and khabar wahid is binding for action.

The incident of the Companions turning in prayer is not only a fiqh ruling but a rich source for usul al-fiqh. The author extracts three critical principles from this single event. Understanding these principles helps us see how Hanafi scholars derive broader legal theories from specific incidents. This lesson focuses on that derivation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Three Principles
The author explicitly states: 'In it is evidence for the permissibility of abrogation of the Book by the Sunnah... and that the ruling of abrogation is not established until it reaches the obligated person, and that the report of one person necessitates action.'
Note: Some commentators correct the first to 'abrogation of Sunnah by the Book' but the author's phrasing is debated.
2
Analyze the First Principle: Abrogation (Naskh)
The initial qibla (Jerusalem) was established by Sunnah (no explicit Qur'anic text). It was later abrogated by the Qur'anic command to face the Ka'ba (2:144). This shows that Sunnah can be abrogated by Qur'an, and by extension, the author holds the reverse is also possible.
Hanafi usul allows mutual naskh between Qur'an and Sunnah, unlike some other schools.
3
Analyze the Second Principle: Notification
The Companions did not change their qibla until they received the news. This proves that the ruling of naskh does not become binding until it reaches the mukallaf.
This is a key Hanafi principle: rulings are not retroactive before notification.
4
Analyze the Third Principle: Binding Nature of Khabar Wahid
The news of the qibla change was brought by a single person (or a few), yet the Companions acted immediately. This demonstrates that a khabar wahid (single-narrator report) is sufficient to necessitate action (wujub al-'amal).
Hanafi: khabar wahid gives zanni knowledge but is binding for practice.
5
Consider the Scholarly Debate
The passage includes a discussion on whether the Jerusalem qibla was established by Qur'an or only by Sunnah. This affects the first principle: if it was from Qur'an, then the abrogation is Qur'an abrogating Qur'an, not Sunnah. The Hanafi position favours Sunnah-only to preserve the principle of naskh al-sunna bi al-kitab.
This debate shows the interaction between fiqh and usul.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله (وإن علم به في صلاته استدار أي إن علم بالخطأ لأن تبدل الاجتهاد بمنزلة تبدل الأنصار كانوا يصلون بمسجد قباء إلى بيت المقدس فأخبروا أن قوماً روي من النسخ وقد بتحول القبلة فاستداروا كهيئتهم. وفيه دليل على جواز نسخ الكتاب السنة إذ لا نص على بيت المقدس في القرآن فعلم أنه كان ثابتاً بالسنة ثم نسخ بالكتاب . وعلى أن حكم النسخ لا يثبت حتى يبلغ المكلف وعلى أن خبر الواحد يوجب العمل. كا ذكر الشارح. وفي كون بيت المقدس ثبت التوجه إليه بالسنة فقط بحث بل في القرآن العاليم ما يدل عليه فإنه قال تعالى سيقول السفهاء من الناس ما ولاهم عن قبلتهم التي كانوا عليها [البقرة: ١٤٢] قال بأن تحول رأيه في كل ركعة إلى جهة غير التي صلى إليها قوله (لأنه لما لم يجز التحري الخ) هذا التعليل غير موافق للمغلل ولعل في العبارة سقطاً فليتأمل . قوله : (بل في القرآن العظيم ما يدل عليه فيه نظر لأنه لا نص على بيت المقدس وإنما السنة بينت أن المراد من قبلتهم بيت المقدس على أن ثبوت التوجه إليه لم يكن حاصلاً بهذه الآية بل كان ثابتاً بالسنة وهذه الآية تدل على نسخه. نعم فيها دلالة بعد البيان على مشروعيته قبلها وليس الكلام في مجرد مشروعيته بل في موجبه وهي لم تدل عليه فليتأمل. كذا قاله الشيخ اسمعيل أقول : وفي الجواب الأول نظر لأن الكتاب إذا بينته السنة يكون الحكم مضافاً إلى الكتاب لا إلى السنة كما نبه عليه في العناية عند الكلام على مسح الرأس. نعم يرد على الشارح الزيلعي أن التوجه إلى بيت المقدس من شرائع من قبلنا وهو ثابت بقوله تعالى
His saying: (And if he knows of it in his prayer, he turns) i.e., if he knows of the error, because the change of ijtihad is like the change of the Ansar. They were praying in the mosque of Quba' towards Jerusalem, and they were informed that some people related that the abrogation had occurred and the qibla had changed, so they turned as they were. In it is evidence for the permissibility of the abrogation of the Book by the Sunnah, since there is no text regarding Jerusalem in the Qur'an, so it is known that it was established by the Sunnah, then abrogated by the Book. And that the ruling of abrogation is not established until it reaches the obligated person, and that the report of one person necessitates action. As the commentator mentioned. And regarding that the direction to Jerusalem was established only by the Sunnah, there is investigation; rather, in the Mighty Qur'an there is indication of it, for He said: 'The fools among the people will say: What has turned them from their qibla which they used to face?' [2:142]. He said: that his opinion changes in every rak'a to a direction other than that to which he prayed. His saying: (Because when taharri is not permitted, etc.) This explanation does not agree with the context, and perhaps there is an omission in the wording, so consider. His saying: (Rather, in the Mighty Qur'an there is indication of it) There is consideration in it, because there is no text regarding Jerusalem; rather, the Sunnah explained that the intended meaning of 'their qibla' is Jerusalem. Moreover, the establishment of the direction to it was not obtained by this verse, but was established by the Sunnah, and this verse indicates its abrogation. Yes, it contains an indication after explanation of its legitimacy before it, and the discussion is not about mere legitimacy but about its obligatoriness, and the verse does not indicate that. So consider. Thus said Sheikh Isma'il. I say: In the first answer there is consideration because the Book, when clarified by the Sunnah, the ruling is attributed to the Book, not to the Sunnah, as pointed out in al-‘Inaya when discussing wiping the head. Yes, there is an objection to the commentator al-Zayla'i that the direction to Jerusalem is from the laws of those before us and is established by the saying of Allah (the Most High).
Source pages p. 503

Commentary
This passage discusses the ruling that if a person realizes during prayer that he is facing the wrong qibla, he should turn to the correct direction immediately without breaking his prayer. The proof is the hadith of the Companions in the mosque of Quba' who were praying towards Jerusalem when they received news that the qibla had been changed to Makkah; they turned around in their prayer. The author also extracts several legal principles from this incident: (1) the Sunnah can abrogate the Qur'an (since the initial qibla was established by Sunnah), (2) abrogation is only effective when it reaches the obligated person, and (3) a single narrator report (khabar wahid) is binding for action. The passage then includes a discussion on whether the direction to Jerusalem was established by the Qur'an or only by Sunnah. The commentator points out that the verse 'Say: To Allah belongs the east and the west' can be interpreted, but the consensus is that it was established by Sunnah and then abrogated by the Qur'anic command to face the Ka'ba. The author also notes an objection by Sheikh Isma'il regarding the attribution of rulings when the Sunnah clarifies the Qur'an—the ruling is then attributed to the Qur'an. The passage ends with a brief critique of al-Zayla'i's commentary.

Key Terms
نسخ (naskh): Abrogation of a previous legal ruling by a later one; here, the change of qibla from Jerusalem to Makkah.
خبر واحد (khabar wāḥid): A single-narrator hadith that is not mutawatir; the passage argues it is binding for action (wujub al-‘amal).
استدارة (istidāra): Turning around to face the correct direction during prayer without breaking it.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT a principle derived from the Quba' hadith according to the passage?
A) The Sunnah can abrogate the Qur'an
B) Abrogation is effective only when it reaches the mukallaf
C) Khabar wahid is not binding for action
D) Khabar wahid obligates action

Key Takeaway
From a single incident, Hanafi usul derives three major principles: mutual naskh between Qur'an and Sunnah, notification condition for naskh, and binding nature of khabar wahid.

Changing Qibla During Prayer: Immediate Turning Upon Knowledge
Ruling on changing qibla during prayer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on turning during prayer when realizing the correct qibla and explain the usul principles derived from the Quba incident.

Core Idea
If you realize the correct qibla during prayer, you must turn immediately—knowledge obligates action.

In this lesson, we explore a pivotal incident from early Islam: the Companions at Masjid Quba turning in prayer upon learning of the qibla change. This event is not merely historical but a foundation for several key usul al-fiqh principles. We will examine how the Hanafi school derives the obligation to turn immediately and what this reveals about the interaction between the Qur'an and Sunnah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
What is the act being ruled upon? (Praying towards the wrong qibla and then realizing the correct one.) What is the ruling? (Must turn immediately.)
The act is the prayer itself, but the ruling specifies the turning.
2
Locate the dalil
Find the proof cited: the hadith of the Ansar at Quba who turned when they received news of the qibla change. This is a khabar wahid (single narration).
The hadith is reported by al-Bukhari and Muslim.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
How does this incident prove the ruling? The Companions turned immediately without waiting, showing that immediate turning is obligatory upon knowledge.
Their action is explicit evidence (dalala qati'a) for the obligation.
4
Extract usul principles
Identify three principles derived: (1) The Sunnah can be abrogated by the Qur'an; (2) The ruling of abrogation takes effect only after it reaches the responsible person; (3) A khabar wahid necessitates action.
These are explicitly mentioned in the commentary.
5
Evaluate the ikhtilaf
Consider the debate: was facing Jerusalem established by Qur'an or Sunnah? The Hanafi position is that it was by Sunnah, not by the Qur'anic verse 'their qibla'.
This affects whether the abrogation is of Sunnah by Qur'an or of Qur'an by Qur'an.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

His statement: 'And if he knows of it during his prayer, he turns' i.e., if he knows of the error, because the change of ijtihad is like the change of the Ansar. They were praying in Masjid Quba towards Bayt al-Maqdis, and they were informed that a people narrated from the abrogation and that the qibla had been changed, so they turned around as they were. And in this is evidence for the permissibility of abrogation of the Sunnah by the Book, since there is no explicit text about Bayt al-Maqdis in the Quran, so it is known that it was established by the Sunnah, then abrogated by the Book. And that the ruling of abrogation is not established until it reaches the responsible person. And that a single narration (khabar wahid) necessitates action, as the commentator mentioned. And regarding the point that facing Bayt al-Maqdis was established only by the Sunnah, there is discussion; rather in the Magnificent Quran there is what indicates it, for He said: 'The fools among the people will say: What has turned them from their qibla which they used to face?' (al-Baqarah 2:142). He said: 'because his opinion changes in every rak'ah to a direction other than the one he prayed towards.' His statement: 'because when taharri is not permissible...' this explanation does not agree with the content, and perhaps there is an omission in the expression, so reflect. Yes, it has indication after explanation of its legality before it, and the discussion is not merely about its legality but about its obligator, and the verse does not indicate that, so reflect. I say: And in the first answer there is consideration, because if the Book is explained by the Sunnah, the ruling is attributed to the Book not to the Sunnah, as was pointed out in al-'Inayah when discussing the wiping of the head. Yes, it refutes the commentator al-Zayla'i that facing Bayt al-Maqdis is from the laws of those before us, and it is established by the saying of Allah.
Source pages p. 503

Commentary
This passage is an expansion probe (sharh) discussing the ruling when a person realizes during prayer that he is facing the wrong qibla. The author comments that one must turn immediately, citing the famous incident of the Companions at Masjid Quba who turned towards the Kaaba when they received news of the qibla change. This is used as evidence for several usul points: (1) The Sunnah can be abrogated by the Quran, since facing Jerusalem was established by Sunnah and abrogated by Quranic verse. (2) The ruling of abrogation takes effect only after the news reaches the person. (3) A single narration (khabar wahid) is sufficient for action. The passage then engages in a critical refinement, debating whether facing Jerusalem was only established by Sunnah or also by the Quran. The commentator argues that the Quranic verse 'their qibla which they used to face' implies it was known, but the obligation was from Sunnah. The discussion includes a refutation of al-Zayla'i's claim that it was from laws of previous prophets, citing the verse 'So follow their guidance' (al-An'am 90) which makes it part of Islamic law. The passage demonstrates the depth of Hanafi usul analysis in fiqh.

Key Terms
نسخ (naskh): Abrogation of a previous ruling by a later revelation; here the abrogation of facing Jerusalem by facing the Kaaba.
خبر واحد (khabar wahid): A narration transmitted by one or a few chains, not reaching tawatur; it necessitates action but not certainty.
قبلة (qibla): The direction Muslims face during prayer; originally Jerusalem, later changed to the Kaaba in Mecca.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) That a ruling of abrogation takes effect only after it reaches the person.
C) That one must complete prayer in the old direction before turning.
D) That facing Jerusalem was established by ijtihad, not revelation.

Key Takeaway
Upon learning the correct qibla during prayer, immediate turning is obligatory, and this incident establishes critical usul principles about naskh and khabar wahid.

The Six Pillars of Prayer and the Definition of Ṣifa
Defining the pillars of prayer and analyzing the term ṣifa

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the six fard pillars of prayer according to the Hanafi school and explain the technical meaning of ṣifa as the intrinsic attributes of prayer.

Core Idea
The six pillars are the essential attributes of prayer; omitting any invalidates it.

This passage opens the chapter on the description of prayer (Bāb Ṣifat al-Ṣalāt). The author immediately lists the six obligatory parts (farā'iḍ) that constitute the prayer’s essential structure. He then delves into a precise linguistic and technical discussion of the term ṣifa itself, clarifying that here it refers to the intrinsic attributes (al-awṣāf al-nafsiyya) of the prayer, which are the intellectual parts corresponding to the external actions. Understanding this distinction is crucial for grasping how Hanafi jurists differentiate between the essential pillars and other components.

Guided Path
1
List the six farḍ pillars
Identify the six components mentioned in the first line: tahrima (opening takbir), qiyam (standing), qira'ah (recitation), ruku' (bowing), sujud (prostration), and qa'dah akhirah (final sitting) for the duration of tashahhud.
The final sitting is a pillar, not merely a sunnah.
2
Understand the ruling level
Recognize that these are farḍ (obligatory) in the Hanafi school. Omission of any invalidates the prayer and requires repetition. Note that Hanafis distinguish farḍ (proven by qat'i dalil) from wājib (proven by zanni dalil).
All six here are farḍ, not wājib.
3
Analyze the term ṣifa linguistically and technically
Follow the author's discussion: linguistically, ṣifa and waṣf can be synonymous but technically differ. Ṣifa is the attribute inherent in the object (mawṣūf), while waṣf is the description uttered. In the context of prayer, ṣifat al-ṣalāt refers to the intrinsic attributes, i.e., the essential parts.
This clarifies that 'ṣifa' here means the real components, not any external description.
4
Apply the six necessities framework
The passage ends with a principle from Sirāj al-Wahhāj: For any thing to be established, six elements are needed: entity (ʿayn), pillar (rukn), ruling (ḥukm), locus (maḥall), condition (sharṭ), and cause (sabab). Relate this to prayer: the prayer is the entity, the pillars are its rukn, the ruling (obligation) is the effect, the body is the locus, purification is a condition, and the entry of time is the cause.
This usul framework grounds the discussion of pillars.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب صفة الصلاة فرضها التحريمة والقيام والقراءة والركوع والسجود والقعود الأخير قدر التشهد شُرع في المقصود بعد الفراغ من مقدماته . قيل : الصفة والوصف في اللغة واحد وفي عرف المتكلمين بخلافه والتحرير أن الوصف لغة ذكر ما في الموصوف من الصفة والصفة هي ما فيه ولا ينكر أنه يطلق الوصف ويراد الصفة وبهذا لا يلزم الاتحاد لغة إذ لا شك في أن الوصف مصدر صفه إذا ذكر ما فيه. ثم المراد هنا بصفة الصلاة الأوصاف النفسية لها وهي الأجزاء العقلية الصادقة على الخارجية التي هي أجزاء الهويه من القيام الجزئي والركوع والسجود. كذا في فتح القدير. وليس هذا من باب قيام العرض بالعرض لأن الأحكام الشرعية لها حكم الجواهر ولهذا توصف بالصحة والفساد والبطلان والفسخ . كذا في غاية البيان. وفي السراج الوهاج ثم اعلم أنه يشترط لثبوت الشيء ستة أشياء العين وهي ماهية الشيء، فلا يكون الشيء، والركن وهو جزء الماهية والحكم وهو الأثر الثابت بالشيء، ومحل ذلك الشيء، وشرطه وسببه فلا يكون الشيء ثابتاً إلا بوجود هذه الأشياء الستة.
Chapter on the Description of Prayer. Its obligatory parts (fard) are: the takbir (tahrima), standing, recitation, bowing, prostration, and the final sitting to the extent of the tashahhud. (The commentator says:) Commencement of the intended topic after finishing its introductions. It was said: sifa and wasf are synonymous in language, and in the terminology of the theologians it is otherwise. The correct view is that wasf (description) in language is the mentioning of what is in the described (mawṣūf) of the attribute (ṣifa), and ṣifa is what is in it. And it cannot be denied that wasf is sometimes used meaning ṣifa, and by this the linguistic identity does not necessitate, for there is no doubt that wasf is a verbal noun (maṣdar) of waṣafa when he mentions what is in it. Then what is meant here by ṣifat al-ṣalāt (description of prayer) are the intrinsic attributes of prayer, which are the intellectual parts that correspond to the external parts that are the parts of its essence: the partial standing, bowing, and prostration. Thus in Fatḥ al-Qadīr. And this is not from the category of the subsistence of an accident by an accident because legal rulings have the status of substances; for this reason they are described as valid, corrupt, void, and annulled. Thus in Ghāyat al-Bayān. And in Sirāj al-Wahhāj: Know that for the establishment of something six things are necessary: 1) the entity (ʿayn) which is the quiddity of the thing, otherwise the thing does not exist; 2) the pillar (rukn) which is part of the quiddity; 3) the legal effect (ḥukm) which is the impact established by the thing; 4) the locus of that thing; 5) its condition (sharṭ); and 6) its cause (sabab). So a thing is not established except by the existence of these six things.
Source pages p. 505

Commentary
This is the opening of the chapter on the description of prayer (Bāb Ṣifat al-Ṣalāt). The author first states the obligatory components (farā'iḍ) of prayer: the takbir (tahrima), standing, recitation, bowing, prostration, and the final sitting for the duration of the tashahhud. Note that the Hanafi school considers these as farḍ (obligatory) and not just wājib; omission of any invalidates the prayer. The commentator then embarks on a linguistic and philosophical discussion of the term 'ṣifa' (description/attribute). He clarifies that linguistically, 'ṣifa' and 'waṣf' can both be used as verbal nouns meaning the act of describing, but technically theologians distinguish: waṣf is the attribute as it resides in the describer (e.g., 'Zaid is knowledgeable'), while ṣifa is the attribute as it resides in the described (e.g., knowledge). However, the intended meaning here of 'ṣifat al-ṣalāt' refers to the essential attributes of prayer, which are the intellectual parts corresponding to the external parts like standing, bowing, prostration. The commentator notes that describing prayer as valid or corrupt does not involve accidents subsisting in accidents because legal rulings have the status of substances. Finally, he cites a principle from Sirāj al-Wahhāj that for any thing to be established, six things are necessary: entity, pillar, ruling, locus, condition, and cause. This is a usul-related note but grounds the discussion of pillars.

Key Terms
صفة (ṣifa): In this context, the intrinsic attributes of prayer, i.e., its essential parts that constitute its reality.
فرض (farḍ): An obligatory component that is essential for the validity of the prayer; omission invalidates it.
التحريمة (al-taḥrīma): The opening takbir (Allahu Akbar) that initiates the prayer and renders prohibited (ḥarām) what was previously permissible.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, how many farḍ pillars are mentioned in this passage for the prayer?
A) Five
B) Six
C) Seven
D) Four

Key Takeaway
The six farḍ pillars—takbir, standing, recitation, bowing, prostration, and final sitting—are the essential attributes (ṣifa) of prayer; their omission invalidates the act.

Validity of Prayer by Ijtihad in the Desert When Opposing the Imam in Qibla
Ruling on the validity of prayer by ijtihad in the desert when qibla direction differs from the imam.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for the validity of a prayer performed by ijtihad in the desert when the qibla is uncertain, and identify when knowledge of opposition to the imam invalidates the prayer.

Core Idea
Prayer in the desert by ijtihad is valid even if you face a different direction than the imam — unless you knew during the prayer.

When traveling through the desert with no one to ask about the qibla, a Muslim may exercise ijtihad (taharrī) to determine the direction of prayer. But what if you and your imam end up facing different directions? This passage from Bahr al-Rā'iq clarifies that the validity depends crucially on when you realize the discrepancy: knowledge during the prayer invalidates it, but knowledge after does not. Understanding this distinction preserves the validity of congregational prayer in wilderness settings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and act
The scenario is praying in the desert (mufāzah) when the qibla is unclear, by personal ijtihad. The act is a congregational prayer with an imam.
2
Recognize the location condition
The ruling explicitly applies only in the desert, not in a village or city. In populated areas, one must ask, not exercise ijtihad.
This condition is a distinctive feature of the Hanafi school.
3
Distinguish timing of knowledge
The key factor is whether the person knew at the time of performance (hāla al-adā') that he was opposing his imam in direction. Knowledge after performance does not affect validity.
The passage emphasizes 'at the time of performance' as the decisive moment.
4
Apply the ruling
If he was certain of opposition during prayer, his prayer is invalid. If he did not know (or only realized later), his prayer is valid.

Source Text

صلوا في المفازة عند اشتباه القبلة بالتحري وتبين أنهم صلوا إلى جهات مختلفة قال: من تيقن مخالفة إمامه في الجهة حالة الأداء لم تجز صلاته، ومن لم يعلم عند الأداء أنه يخالف إمامه في الجهة فصلاته صحيحة فشرط أن يكون في المفازة وهو يدل على أن التحري لا يجوز في القرية والمصر من غير سؤال وقد أسلفناه، وأفاد أن علمه بالمخالفة بعد الأداء لا يضر والله أعلم.
They prayed in the desert when the qibla was uncertain by ijtihad, and it became apparent that they had prayed in different directions. He said: Whoever is certain of opposing his imam in direction at the time of performance, his prayer is not valid. And whoever did not know at the time of performance that he opposed his imam in direction, his prayer is valid. The condition is that it be in the desert, and this indicates that ijtihad is not permissible in the village and city without asking. And we have already mentioned this. And it benefits that his knowledge of opposition after performance does not harm. And Allah knows best.

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling about prayer in the desert when the qibla is uncertain. It states that if a person is certain during prayer that he is facing a different direction than his imam, his prayer is invalid. However, if he only realizes this after completing the prayer, his prayer remains valid. The ruling is conditioned on being in a desert (mufaza), which indicates that in a village or city one must ask about the qibla and cannot resort to ijtihad without asking. This is a ruling from the Hanafi school, and the passage clarifies that knowledge of opposition after the fact does not affect validity.

Key Terms
التحري (al-taḥarrī): Exercising ijtihad to determine the qibla when the direction is uncertain
المفازة (al-mufāzah): Wilderness or desert where there are no signs or people to ask about qibla
الإمام (al-imām): The leader in congregational prayer
